Source: Architectural Blueprints for Highly Resilient Application Delivery: From L4 Transport to L7 Distributed Session Coordination
Type: Pasted Text
Architectural Blueprints for Highly Resilient Application Delivery: From L4 Transport to L7 Distributed Session CoordinationLayer 4 Transport Architecture and Performance OptimizationThis section analyzes the transport layer mechanisms required to establish highly resilient application delivery networks, focusing on high-speed packet forwarding, kernel-level socket memory optimizations, transport-agnostic proxy decoupling, and boundary security enforcement.High-Packet Forwarding MechanicsEstablishing line-rate Layer 4 (L4) forwarding requires minimizing packet traversal latency through the operating system network stack.[1] Traditional L4 load-balancing proxy implementations running in user-space suffer from significant processing overhead, as every incoming packet must cross the user-kernel boundary twice, executing context switches and copying data buffer payloads.[1] To circumvent this bottleneck, architectures rely on high-speed packet processing frameworks, primarily the Data Plane Development Kit (DPDK) and the Express Data Path (XDP) with eBPF.[2, 3]DPDK operates on a user-space kernel-bypass model.[3] By detaching the network interface card (NIC) from the operating system kernel, DPDK assumes full control over the hardware, utilizing Poll Mode Drivers (PMDs) to continuously poll the NIC for incoming packets.[2, 3] This continuous polling eliminates the overhead of hardware interrupts, making DPDK highly efficient for single-buffer packet processing workloads.[2, 3] However, this model consumes 100% of the allocated CPU cores regardless of traffic volume.[3, 4] Furthermore, because DPDK bypasses the kernel completely, it lacks access to standard kernel network objects and namespaces, requiring a complete reimplementation of the network stack for complex Layer 7 (L7) functions.[3, 5, 6]XDP, conversely, integrates directly with the Linux kernel.[3, 7] Operating at the lowest driver level before the kernel allocates socket buffer structures, XDP executes eBPF programs within the NIC driver context.[3] This in-kernel execution provides strictly bounded, loop-free execution times.[3] XDP scales linearly with the number of NIC receive (Rx) queues.[2, 4] Because each queue invokes the eBPF program independently in a lockless, per-CPU map configuration, XDP provides excellent multi-buffer packet processing performance.[2, 4]In cloud-native and virtualized environments, the choice of forwarding framework is highly dependent on system load.[8] High-throughput testing under co-located CPU, I/O, and network contention reveals that DPDK is approximately 5% to 10% faster than XDP due to its aggressive polling mechanics.[3] However, under heavy network loads with small-sized packets, both frameworks experience throughput degradation and packet loss in virtualized configurations.[8]Despite this, XDP-based architectures like Katran achieve extreme throughput, processing three times the packets of standard IPVS setups with seven times less CPU utilization, while allowing the hosting server to run standard container workloads in parallel.[4, 9]Kernel Line Memory and Socket TuningFor load balancers handling massive concurrent connections, default operating system kernel configurations are too conservative.[11] A server processing over one million packets per second must optimize kernel socket line memory to prevent socket buffer exhaustion and packet drops, which are measured using the nstat UdpRcvbufErrors counter.[11]The primary tuning parameters target the maximum and default socket receive (SO_RCVBUF) and send (SO_SNDBUF) limits.[11, 12] System configurations must explicitly raise net.core.rmem_max and net.core.wmem_max to allow applications to request larger buffers.[11, 12] The default buffer sizes, before application-level overrides, are configured via net.core.rmem_default and net.core.wmem_default.[11] Sizing these buffers must align with the Bandwidth-Delay Product (BDP) to prevent connection stalling [12]: \text{BDP} = \text{Bandwidth} \times \text{RTT}In addition to socket buffer allocation, the network device input queue (net.core.netdev_max_backlog) must be expanded to prevent packet drops between the NIC and the socket layer.[11] The NAPI polling budget (net.core.netdev_budget) should be increased to allow the kernel to process more packets per polling cycle.[11] Physical NIC ring buffers must be scaled to their hardware maxima using ethtool.[11] For ultra-low-latency transport, busy polling (net.core.busy_poll) can be enabled, forcing the CPU to spin-wait for incoming packets for a defined period, eliminating the 10 to 50 microsecond latency of hardware interrupts at the cost of high CPU utilization.[11]To scale connection acceptance across multi-core systems, the SO_REUSEPORT socket option is critical.[12, 13] Historically introduced in Linux 3.9 by Tom Herbert, this option allows multiple listening sockets to bind to the exact same IP and port combination.[10, 13] The kernel then distributes incoming connections across the independent accept queues of the bound sockets using a 4-tuple or round-robin hash.[10, 12] This avoids the severe thundering herd problem and lock contention associated with multiple threads competing for a single shared socket lock.[10, 14] Under standard shared-accept designs, connection distribution imbalances across worker threads can reach a 3:1 ratio.[10] Enabling SO_REUSEPORT yields uniform traffic distribution across worker threads.[10, 14]In Go-based load balancers, where socket options are not exposed natively in the standard library, developers inject this option by manipulating raw file descriptors during the socket's control lifecycle [12]:Further evolutionary enhancements introduced by Martin KaFai Lau in Linux 4.19 utilize the BPF_PROG_TYPE_SK_REUSEPORT program type.[10] This mechanism allows an attached eBPF program to select target sockets directly from a BPF_MAP_TYPE_REUSEPORT_ARRAY map, rather than relying on standard kernel hashing.[10] This provides high programmatic flexibility during rolling upgrades and graceful restarts, allowing a new proxy process to insert its sockets into the map and drain old sockets without dropping transit packets.[10]Network-Agnostic Generic DecouplingTo achieve extreme resilience, Layer 4 architectures must maintain a generic, network-agnostic forwarding plane, decoupled from Layer 7 application contexts.[4] The forwarder operates strictly on packet headers, utilizing 5-tuple hash calculations to track connections without parsing application payloads.[1, 4]A prominent design pattern, exemplified by the XLB socket enhancement architecture, extends standard Linux sockets with two optimized types: proxy sockets (p-sock) and instance sockets (i-sock).[5] The client-side connection binds to a p-sock which encapsulates the core load-balancing and routing logic, while pre-established connection pools of i-socks are maintained directly with backend servers.[5] XLB coordinates lightweight message redirection directly between these sockets in kernel-space using eBPF interposition, avoiding the massive memory structures required by traditional connection tracking engines.[5]To support concurrent requests over multiplexed protocols (such as HTTP/2), the i-sock maintains an internal request map.[5] For in-order protocols like HTTP/1.1, the proxy holds subsequent requests until the preceding response is received.[5] For multiplexed protocols, the proxy dynamically maps internal request identifiers (e.g., HTTP/2 stream IDs) to their original client-side values.[5] To preserve isolation boundaries, connection pools are isolated within container cgroups.[5]Forwarded packets are encapsulated using IP-in-IP (IPIP) or Geneve protocols.[4, 15] This encapsulation is designed to be Receive Side Scaling (RSS) friendly; by generating outer IP header source fields that hash to different CPU queues on the receiving NIC, the load balancer ensures even load distribution across L7 backend processing cores.[4]Decoupled Client Details AuthenticationA critical design requirement for resilient architectures is the isolation of security boundaries.[16] The transport proxy terminates client authentication at the edge, abstracting client identities and preventing downstream application microservices from participating in direct security handshakes.[16, 17] Mutual TLS (mTLS) is the industry standard for this boundary authentication, mandating that both client and server present X.509 certificates to verify cryptographic identity.[16, 18]The mTLS handshake consists of six key stages: the client initiates with a Client Hello containing cipher suites [18]; the proxy responds with its certificate and a CertificateRequest [18]; the client validates the proxy's certificate chain [18]; the client submits its certificate accompanied by a CertificateVerify message containing a digital signature over the handshake transcript [18]; the proxy validates the client's signature, chain of trust, and temporal validity [16, 18]; and both parties derive session keys to establish a secure channel.[18]The validation checks performed by the proxy are exhaustive:Proof of Possession: The proxy verifies the client signature using the public key embedded in the client certificate, confirming private key ownership.[16, 18]Chain of Trust Verification: The proxy traces the certificate chain to a trusted root authority, checking that the Parent Subject matches the Child Issuer, and matching the Subject Key Identifier (SKID) of the parent with the Authority Key Identifier (AKID) of the child.[16, 19] Self-signed certificates are rejected.[16]Temporal and Extension Audits: The proxy ensures the current time falls within the certificate's notBefore and notAfter bounds, and enforces Extended Key Usage (EKU) constraints, verifying the client certificate contains clientAuth permissions.[19]Once validated, the client's identity is abstracted and decoupled.[16] The proxy injects the validated client metadata, such as the Subject Alternative Name (SAN) or SPIFFE ID, into custom HTTP headers or forwards them via the PROXY protocol before routing the request to backend servers.[16, 19] This edge termination shields internal services from brute-force, credential-stuffing, on-path, and spoofing attacks, and eliminates the need for applications to manage complex cryptographic handshakes.[17, 18] To manage this at scale, certificate lifecycles are automated using tools like cert-manager, HashiCorp Vault, or SPIRE, enforcing short validity windows (under 24 hours).[18]Layer 7 Application Delivery and Intelligent RoutingLayer 7 application proxies conduct deep packet inspection, terminate secure sessions, and apply routing logic based on application-level metadata.High-Performance HTTP/S Processing and ParsingDeep packet inspection at Layer 7 introduces significant CPU overhead due to cross-boundary memory copying.[20] In mainstream proxies, copying data from kernel socket buffers to user-space buffers consumes substantial CPU cycles as payload sizes increase [20, 21]:\text{Nginx Copy CPU Consumption} = \begin{cases} 15.9% & \text{for } 16 \text{ KB payloads} \ 55.6% & \text{for } 256 \text{ KB payloads} \end{cases} [20, 21]Similar bottlenecks affect HAProxy (39.4%), Traefik (33.3%), and Envoy (24.6%).[20, 21] Profiling reveals a stark receive-to-transmit (Rx/Tx) copy asymmetry, where Rx copying is over four times more expensive than Tx copying.[20, 21] On the receive path, the NIC writes packets into scattered, discontiguous driver pages via DMA, forcing the kernel's copy_to_user routine to perform scattered reads, defeating CPU prefetching and triggering high Translation Lookaside Buffer (TLB) miss rates (~0.40%).[21] On the transmit path, copy_from_user packages data into contiguous, pre-allocated high-order pages, keeping the TLB miss rate ten times lower.[21]To bypass this conflict, modern L7 proxies use zero-copy parsers like picohttpparser or httparse, achieving processing throughputs over 2.5 GB/s with zero memory allocation in the hot path.[22, 23] High-performance proxies like Meridian employ lock-free configurations (e.g., arc-swap), array-indexed counters instead of HashMaps, and loop timestamps to eliminate systemic overhead.[23]To achieve zero-copy while maintaining POSIX compatibility, the Libra framework separates L7 policy from kernel memory mechanisms using eBPF state machines (RX-Prog and TX-Prog).[20, 21] The proxy's recv system call triggers RX-Prog, which parses headers, copies only L7 metadata to user space, and leaves the bulk payload anchored in the kernel receive buffer.[20] It then generates an opaque 64-bit Virtual Payload Identifier (VPI) mapped to the socket descriptor in a global eBPF hash table, which is injected into user space in place of the payload.[20]On transmit, TX-Prog intercepts the write stream, extracts the VPI, retrieves the original socket context, and performs a pointer manipulation to transfer payload ownership, avoiding cross-boundary memory moves.[20] This architecture improves plaintext throughput by up to 4.2 times and cuts P99 latency by over 90%.[21] Similarly, ModPageSpeed 2.0 architectures serve cache hits via zero-copy mmap mapping, preventing synchronous encoding delays.[24]SSL / TLS TerminationTerminating TLS at the L7 proxy edge is a compute-intensive operation.[26, 27] In traditional setups, encrypted records are copied to user-space libraries (like OpenSSL), decrypted, and copied back to kernel space for transmission.[28] This is optimized by Kernel TLS (kTLS), which moves the TLS record layer directly into the kernel TCP Upper Layer Protocol (ULP) layer.[28, 29] When kTLS is paired with Nginx's SSL_sendfile(), static files are encrypted directly within kernel space before network transmission, eliminating user-space memory copies and doubling Nginx throughput.[29, 30]Under modern kernel configurations, kTLS operates in three primary modes [27]:Software (SW) Mode: All handshake, record framing, encryption, and decryption are handled natively within kernel software modules, saving 20% to 40% CPU overhead over user-space OpenSSL.[27, 30]Hardware Offload (HW-Offload) Mode: The handshake and session key negotiation are completed in software, but the symmetric encryption and decryption of raw packet payloads are offloaded directly to inline SmartNIC hardware (such as Chelsio T6 or NVIDIA ConnectX-6 Dx / ConnectX-7).[27, 31] Inline TLS offload delivers a 2x throughput boost over software kTLS.[30]Hardware Record (HW-Record) Mode: Fully offloads the entire TLS session, including handshakes and TCP session management, directly to the SmartNIC hardware.[27]Deployments utilizing inline offloads with a TCP Offloading Engine (TOE) or Coprocessor acceleration configurations achieve massive scale, reducing memory bandwidth utilization by 50% and supporting 400 to 800 Gb/s per server in production environments.[30, 31]Path-Based RoutingOnce requests are decrypted and parsed, the proxy maps them to the appropriate upstream service.[25] Evaluating routes via sequential linear scans scales at O(N) time complexity, which becomes a severe bottleneck under thousands of route definitions.[25] To guarantee high resilience, proxies like Nginx and Envoy construct an in-memory Radix Tree (Patricia Trie) that compresses common URI path prefixes.[25]The lookup complexity of a Radix Tree is bounded to O(K), where K is the length of the requested URI path, making lookup performance entirely independent of the total number of registered routes (N).[25] This structure allows proxies to match incoming paths (e.g., /api/v1/users/42) character-group by character-group to resolve destination endpoints within sub-microsecond windows.[25]Smart / Feature-Based RoutingSmart routing extends path-based matching by evaluating application-level metadata, including request cookies, headers, query parameters, connection source IPs, and filter states.[32] To execute this dynamically without proxy restarts, modern control planes decouple configuration management from the data forwarding plane.[25] The control plane stores routes in a centralized datastore, and a Config Distribution Service broadcasts dynamic updates across the proxy cluster using a Redis Pub/Sub channel.[25]Within five seconds of an administrative change, proxy instances update their local in-memory Radix Trees.[25] This real-time synchronization supports advanced capabilities like weight-based canary routing (splitting traffic from 0% to 100% to a canary service) and dynamic headers injection without interrupting active connections.[25]TLS Performance ScalingWhile symmetric encryption is offloaded via kTLS, the initial asymmetric key exchange (RSA/ECDSA handshakes) remains a computationally expensive operation.[26, 33] Standard TLS implementations in proxies use synchronous BoringSSL handshakes, which block the proxy's worker event threads during cryptographic calculations, stalling the entire system.[26, 34]To scale asymmetric processing, proxies deploy the "private key provider" extension.[26, 34] This utilizes BoringSSL private key hooks, allowing ECDSA sign and RSA decrypt operations to return immediately while offloading the cryptography to external hardware accelerators, such as Intel QuickAssist Technology (QAT) or vector-optimized instructions like CryptoMB (Intel AVX-512).[26, 34] Once the hardware completes the operation, a callback is triggered to finalize the handshake.[26, 34]Integrating Intel QAT within a containerized Kubernetes environment requires several prerequisites [35]:Kernel and Drivers: Install Linux kernel 5.17 or later with Intel IOMMU enabled (intel_iommu=on).[35]Virtualization Mapping: Expose the physical QAT PCIe device as Single Root I/O Virtualization Virtual Functions (SR-IOV VFs), binding them to the vfio-pci driver.[35]Resource Constraints: Raise the container engine's memory lock limit (LimitMEMLOCK in containerd) to at least 134,217,728 bytes, allowing the driver to pin memory for DMA transfers.[35]Polling Configuration: Tune the proxy's QAT polling delay (poll_delay, with a recommended default of 2 milliseconds) to balance CPU consumption against latency requirements.[26, 34]When configured correctly, QAT hardware offload increases handshake throughput by up to five times while reducing host CPU utilization by 30 percentage points, allowing the proxy's saved CPU cycles to be utilized for high-throughput L7 HTTP filter processing.[26, 34]WAF Integration LogicProtecting L7 applications from vulnerabilities like the OWASP Top 10 requires integrating Web Application Firewalls (WAF).[36] Standard deployments rely on inline WAF proxies (such as WAF 2.0 transparent gateways).[37] These inline proxies inspect full HTTP payloads in the direct datapath, creating significant network latency, complex certificate synchronization requirements, and a dangerous single point of failure (SPOF).[37, 38]To achieve high resilience, modern application delivery networks deploy the WAF 3.0 SDK integration model.[37] This model completely decouples security inspection from traffic forwarding.[37] When the proxy receives an HTTP request, it uses an embedded SDK to synchronously extract and mirror only the request metadata to an out-of-band security inspection cluster, allowing the forwarding path to scale independently.[37]For passive traffic monitoring, hardware-level bypass TAPs (such as multimode or singlemode optical splitters, or active fail-safe copper bypass switches) copy and redirect traffic to out-of-band monitoring appliances.[38, 39] If an inline tool fails, the bypass TAP automatically routes traffic around it to maintain availability.[38, 39]For virtualized cloud networks, VPC packet mirroring replicates traffic streams, encapsulating them using Geneve to preserve original packet structures and overlapping IP spaces.[15]Traffic Distribution and Consistent Hashing AlgorithmsHighly resilient load balancing depends on balancing algorithms that prevent backend server hotspotting and maintain consistent routing structures.Standard Distribution AlgorithmsProxies use traditional scheduling algorithms to distribute connections across target pools [32, 40]:Round Robin: Distributes requests sequentially across eligible backends.[32, 40] It is highly efficient for homogeneous pools but can cause hotspotting if tasks vary in execution time.[32, 40]Least Connections: Routes traffic to the backend with the lowest number of active sessions.[40] This dynamic, adaptive scheduling is ideal for heterogeneous pools and automatically bypasses slow or degraded servers.[5, 40]IP Hash: Computes a hash of the client's IP address to map requests to a dedicated backend, providing basic session persistence.[13, 32]To handle unequal server capabilities, proxies use weighted versions of these algorithms.[4, 41] Weighted Round Robin and Weighted Least Connections route connections in proportion to pre-allocated host weights, ensuring that low-capacity servers are not overwhelmed.[4, 41]Consistent Hashing: Ring Hash vs. MaglevFor large-scale, distributed environments, standard hashing algorithms are inadequate because any change in backend pool membership (scaling up or down) invalidates the hash space, causing massive session termination.[40, 42] To prevent this, architectures implement consistent hashing.[42] The two primary consistent hashing algorithms are Ring Hash (Ketama) and Maglev.[41, 42]The Ring Hash algorithm maps both the upstream host addresses and the request keys onto a 360-degree coordinate circle (the "ring").[32, 41] Each host is hashed and placed on the ring multiple times proportional to its weight, with the minimum ring size configurable from 1,024 to 8,000,000 entries.[41, 42] A request is routed to the nearest host located clockwise from the request key's hash position.[32, 41]Ring Hash is exceptionally stable; when a host is removed, only the keys mapped to that host are redistributed, leaving the rest of the pool unaffected.[40] However, Ring Hash introduces O(\log(\text{Ring Size})) search latency and generates massive lookup tables that can escape CPU cache lines, adding latency at scale.[40]Google's Maglev algorithm resolves this latency by using a fixed lookup table of prime size (typically 65,537, but scalable up to 5,000,011).[32, 41] Each host uses a pseudorandom permutation to populate its preferred slots in the shared table.[41, 43] This design enables O(1) constant-time request routing, delivering 10x faster table build times and 5x faster host selection times than Ring Hash.[41]The primary drawback of Maglev is its stability; when host membership changes, approximately twice as many keys move compared to Ring Hash.[41] In Kubernetes environments, where pods are highly transient, this frequent redistribution makes Maglev expensive.[40] Ring Hash remains superior for stateful application environments where minimizing key movement is critical.[40, 41]To optimize consistent hashing, proxies like Envoy support multiple hash policies (such as cookie, query parameter, header, or connection source IP).[32] These policies can be configured with a terminal attribute; if a terminal policy succeeds, the proxy short-circuits subsequent evaluations, saving valuable CPU cycles in the hot routing path.[32, 42]Distributed Session Management and Coordination ModelsManaging session state across highly resilient, horizontally scaled application delivery networks requires robust synchronization models.Session Affinity and Centralized CachingMaintaining session state across multiple servers relies on either soft session affinity (using consistent hashing) or strong sticky sessions.[42] Strong sticky sessions encode the target backend address in an injected cookie or HTTP header.[42] The proxy inspects this token on subsequent requests to route the client to the same backend.[42]While sticky sessions are simple to implement, they create significant architectural risks: if a backend instance fails, its local session state is lost, disrupting active users.[13, 42] Furthermore, scaling down backend pools forces session terminations on the removed instances.[42]To build a resilient architecture, systems decouple state from compute.[13, 25] This pattern utilizes a centralized, highly available in-memory data store like Redis to store session state, allowing the L7 proxy and application tiers to remain completely stateless.[13, 25] If a backend node fails or is scaled down, the new target node can instantly retrieve the user's session from the centralized cache, ensuring uninterrupted session continuity across pool changes and rolling upgrades.[13, 25]Distributed Coordination and Session Clustering (The CONGEST Model)When session storage cannot be centralized—such as in geographically dispersed, multi-region edge clusters—the system must coordinate state across distributed nodes.[44] In theoretical computer science, this is modeled as a distributed graph coordination problem.[45, 46] In these environments, communication bandwidth is the primary bottleneck.[46, 47] This constraint is formalized in the CONGEST model.[45, 47]The CONGEST model abstracts a communication network as an n-node graph of maximum degree \Delta.[45, 48] Communication proceeds in synchronous rounds, where each node can send a message of at most O(\log n) bits over any given link per round.[45, 47] This is more restrictive than the LOCAL model, which allows unlimited bandwidth.[47, 49]In a k-clique message passing model, a vertex-partitioned graph of n vertices is distributed among k players communicating over bidirectional links.[45] In every round, each player performs local computation and sends at most O(\beta \log n) bits of data, receiving at most O(\beta k \log n) bits.[45] When k=n and \beta=1, this model is equivalent to the Congested Clique model.[45] Under this framework, executing dynamic session additions or deletions is represented as finding a maximal matching under batch-dynamic edge updates.[45, 50]In a Congested Clique network, computing distributed graph optimization problems requires managing strict communication limits.[45, 46] For any network topology, the minimum number of rounds required for distributed optimization is bounded by a universal lower limit [51]:\text{Distributed Optimization Lower Bound} = \tilde{\Omega}(\sqrt{n}) \text{ rounds } [51]This lower bound is mathematically characterized by general shortcuts of best congestion + dilation.[51]When coordinating state in extremely harsh environments, such as a Beeping Network (BN)—where nodes can only transmit a single beep or listen to detect silence or noise—communication costs increase significantly.[52] Simulating a single round of any CONGEST algorithm in a Beeping Network requires a multiplicative overhead of O(\Delta^2 \text{polylog } n) rounds deterministically, or O(\Delta^2 \log n) rounds in a noisy beeping model.[48, 52] This mathematical relationship demonstrates how restricting communication bandwidth dramatically increases the rounds of message passing required to achieve distributed synchronization.[48, 52]To coordinate state across networks with active security threats, compilers must tolerate adversarial nodes.[53] Implementing a general round-by-round compiler against f adversarial edges (which can inject malicious or corrupt messages) introduces a compilation overhead of CO(D^{O(f)}) rounds, where D is the network diameter.[53]To bypass the runtime limits of traditional distributed consensus, modern architectures leverage Graph Neural Network Variational Autoencoders (GNN-VAE).[44] Rather than executing slow, multi-round coordination algorithms, GNN-VAEs formulate coordination as a graph optimization problem, training on ground-truth solutions from Mixed-Integer Linear Programming (MILP) solvers.[44]During training, the GNN-VAE encodes high-quality graph solutions into a low-dimensional latent space.[44] At inference time, distributed edge nodes decode these latent variables locally, selecting the lowest-cost coordination path.[44] This approach matches the accuracy of centralized methods and distributes the computational complexity across the cluster, allowing session clustering models to converge faster and bypass traditional communication bottlenecks.[44]

Source: Beeping Deterministic CONGEST Algorithms in Graphs - DROPS
URL: https://drops.dagstuhl.de/storage/00lipics/lipics-vol351-esa2025/LIPIcs.ESA.2025.20/LIPIcs.ESA.2025.20.pdf
Beeping Deterministic CONGEST Algorithms in Graphs Pawel Garncarek # Institute of Computer Science, University of Wroclaw, Poland Dariusz R. Kowalski # School of Computer & Cyber Sciences, Augusta University, GA, USA Shay Kutten # Department of Data and Decision Sciences, Technion, Haifa, Israel The Grand Technion Energy Program, Haifa, Israel Miguel A. Mosteiro # Pace University, Computer Science Department, New York, NY, USA Abstract Beeping Network (BN) is a popular graph-based model of wireless computation, which applies the OR operation to one-bit messages sent simultaneously by neighbors. It admits fast (polylogarithmic in the number of nodes n) randomized solutions to many graph problems, but all known deterministic algorithms for non-trivial graph problems are at least polynomial in the maximum node degree ∆. We improve known results for deterministic algorithms by showing that this polynomial can be as low as Õ(∆2). More precisely, we show how to simulate a single round of any CONGEST algorithm in any network in O(∆2 polylog n) beeping rounds, each accommodating at most one beep per node, even if the nodes intend to send different messages to different neighbors. This upper bound reduces polynomially the time for a deterministic simulation of CONGEST in a Beeping Network, comparing to the best known algorithms, and nearly matches the time obtained recently using randomization (up to a poly-logarithmic factor) as well as the lower bound. Specifically, any algorithm designed for the CONGEST networks can be run in BNs with O(∆2 polylog n) multiplicative overhead, e.g., we can now deterministically compute an MIS in any BN in O(∆2 polylog n) beeping rounds, improving the previous best Θ(∆3)-round solution. For h-hop simulations, we prove a lower bound Ω(∆h+1), and we design a nearly matching algorithm that is able to “pipeline” the node-to-node information in a faster way than beeping layer-by-layer. 2012 ACM Subject Classification Theory of computation → Distributed algorithms Keywords and phrases Beeping Networks, CONGEST Networks, deterministic simulations, graph algorithms Digital Object Identifier 10.4230/LIPIcs.ESA.2025.20 Related Version Full Version: https://arxiv.org/abs/2502.13424 [19] Funding Pawel Garncarek: Supported by the National Science Center, Poland (NCN), grant 2020/39/B/ST6/03288. Shay Kutten: The work of this author was funded in part by grant 1346/22 from the Israeli Science Foundation. His research was carried out within the framework of the Grand Technion Energy Program (GTEP). A part of this work was done while with Fraunhofer SIT Darmstadt. Miguel A. Mosteiro: Partially supported by Pace SRC grant and Kenan Fund. 1 Introduction The study of the Beeping Networks (BN) model [10] is simultaneously interesting, challenging, and useful in several respects. Even some less-restrictive ad-hoc networks (e.g., wireless) are frequently the algorithmist delight: a seemingly simple computational problem that © Pawel Garncarek, Dariusz R. Kowalski, Shay Kutten, and Miguel A. Mosteiro; licensed under Creative Commons License CC-BY 4.0 33rd Annual European Symposium on Algorithms (ESA 2025). Editors: Anne Benoit, Haim Kaplan, Sebastian Wild, and Grzegorz Herman; Article No. 20; pp. 20:1–20:17 Leibniz International Proceedings in Informatics Schloss Dagstuhl – Leibniz-Zentrum für Informatik, Dagstuhl Publishing, Germany 20:2 Beeping Deterministic CONGEST Algorithms in Graphs becomes challenging under harsh yet realistic conditions. In terms of communication, BN may be the harshest model: network nodes can only beep (emit a signal) or listen (detect if a signal is emitted in its vicinity). A listening node may distinguish between silence (no beeps) and noise (at least one beep), but it cannot distinguish between single and multiple beeps. Theoretically, the BN model is important since it enables one to study whether distributed tasks can be performed efficiently under minimal conditions. It is a fundamental model to study communication complexity on channels where signals are superimposed (by applying the OR operator), which makes it more challenging than the basic model in which transmissions on links are independent. Moreover, in a one-hop network,1 non-adaptive communication schedules in the BN model are equivalent to superimposed codes, which have been widely applied not only to communication problems, but also to text alignments, bio-computing, data pooling, dimensionality reduction, and other areas. In addition to its theoretical importance, this model is recognized as useful for studying natural communication in biological networks [30, 2] (e.g., cells). A better understanding of the power of biological communication processes may lead to better nature-inspired algorithms [28]. It has been argued that the model is also a practical tool because algorithms designed for BNs can be implemented on non-expensive devices with low energy consumption. For example, nodes deployed (in ad-hoc topologies) for monitoring in Internet of Things (IoT) applications such as Sensor Networks. It should be noted that a mechanism similar in properties to beeping, called “busy tone,” has been in wide use in wireless networks but for very limited purposes in channel access algorithms (as opposed to the Beeping model that is intended for general-purpose distributed algorithms). See e.g., [32, 22]. A wealth of successful research on computational problems in Beeping Networks has appeared in the literature (see, for instance [5, 15] and the references therein). Nevertheless, fundamental distributed computing questions remain open in the context of Beeping Networks, especially for deterministic algorithms. On the other hand, the CONGEST [31] model has been profusely studied. A natural question that follows is how to efficiently transform CONGEST Network algorithms into Beepping Network algorithms. 1.1 Our Contributions The main results are detailed and compared to previous work in Table 1. Our main contributions are the near optimal2 deterministic implementations of two simulators. The main simulator, see Section 3 and Theorem 8, efficiently translates any CONGEST algorithms (deterministic or randomized) to the Beeping model. Each CONGEST round is simulated using O(∆2 polylog n) rounds, improving the previous result of O(∆4 log n) of [5] and recent O(∆3 log n) of [12] 3 (for ∆ ∈ ω( polylog n)), and matches the lower bound Ω(∆2 log n) in [12] (that lower bound holds even for randomized simulations, hence combined with our algorithm they imply that the randomization can not help more than, possibly, a polylogarithmic factor). Our approach differs substantially from previous ones based on superimposed codes. Our algorithm is adaptive, but using a family of codes, called avoiding selectors, that are parts of nodes’ beeping schedules. These codes combined assure that, gradually, more and more 1 For any given path of links connecting two nodes, the number of hops is the number of links in such path. 2 Optimal up to a polylogarithmic factor. 3 The latter result was not explicitly stated in [12], but one could use its randomized O(∆2 log n)-round solution designed for broadcasting O(∆ log n) input bits stored in each node (this is the number of bits in our case, as messages to individual ∆ neighbors could be different), and then derandomize it by using the suggested superimposed codes (with additional factor O(∆), hence O(∆3 log n) in total). P. Garncarek, D. R. Kowalski, S. Kutten, and M. A. Mosteiro 20:3 Table 1 Summary of our main results and related work for Beeping Networks with n nodes and maximum degree ∆. All protocols are distributed. Highlighted pairs of cells show direct comparison of our main results with previous work. B denotes the maximum number of input bits at a node. problem protocol type beeping rounds ref simulation of one CONGEST round randomized O(∆ min(n, ∆2) log n) whp [3] O(∆2 log n) whp [12] deterministic O(∆4 log n) [5] O(∆3 log n) [12] O(∆2 polylog n) Thm. 8 rand./det. Ω(∆2 log n) [12] B-bit h-hop simulation deterministic O(h ·B∆h+2 polylog n) Thm. 11 rand./det. Ω(B∆h+1) Thm. 10 Learning Neighborhood deterministic O(∆2 log2 n) in full version of this work [19] Cluster Gathering O(∆2 log4 n) in full version of this work [19] (log n, log2 n)-Network Decomposition O(∆2 log8 n) in full version of this work [19] MIS O(∆3 + ∆2 log n) [5] O(∆2 polylog n) Cor. 9 links will “successfully beep” the information between their two end nodes, by “avoiding” interfering beeps from other nodes. The main challenge is that each node may have some incident links already successful and other links that are not – hence, it has to beep anyways and may interfere with other neighbors but, thanks to our avoiding selectors, not all the time. In this process, once a node recognizes that a neighbor is a unique beeper (so called “announcer”), it decodes its ID and message, and then it competes with other nodes to respond in subsequent rounds, and after that – it gets confirmation from the announcer. Simulations of some CONGEST algorithms, relying on sending the same message to all neighbors in a round such as Network Decomposition in [20], could be further improved by a polylogarithmic factor by applying a deterministic version of local broadcast algorithm (i.e., same message to all neighbors) proposed in [12] (see the full version of this work [19]). Our second main result is an extension of the notion of neighborhood to h ≥ 1 hops and pipelining of the one-hop simulation. We call this problem a B-bit h-hop simulation, and show bounds O(h ·B∆h+2 polylog n) and Ω(B∆h+1), where the lower bound holds also for randomized solutions. See Section 4, and Theorems 11 and 10, respectively. Our algorithm efficiently “pipelines” point-to-point messages, and achieves substantially better complexity (for h > 3) than a straightforward application of 1-hop simulator h times (which would need O(∆2h polylog n) time). For the simpler problem when each node has to deliver the same message to all nodes in its h-hop neighborhood, which we call B-bit h-hop Local Broadcast, we show a lower bound of Ω(B∆h) even with randomization (see the full version of this work [19]). ESA 2025 20:4 Beeping Deterministic CONGEST Algorithms in Graphs 1.2 Related Work The Beeping Network model was defined by Cornejo and Kuhn in [10] in 2010, inspired by continuous beeping studied by Degesys et al. [13] and Motskin et al. [29], and by the implementation of coordination by carrier sensing given by Flury and Wattenhofer in [17]. Since then, the literature has included studies on MIS and Coloring [2, 1, 27, 23, 5, 7], Naming [8], Leader Election [21, 18, 14], Broadcast [21, 25, 26, 11, 4], and Shortest Paths [15]. Techniques to implement CONGEST algorithms in BNs were studied. In [5], the approach is to schedule transmissions according to a 2-hop c-coloring to avoid collisions. The mul-tiplicative overhead introduced by the simulation is O(c2 log n). A constant c is enough for the simulation, but the only coloring algorithm provided in the same paper takes time O(a2∆2 log2 n + a3∆3 log n) for a (∆2 + 1)-coloring. Thus, the multiplicative overhead is O(∆4 log n), which our simulation improves by a factor of ∆2/ polylog n. On the side of randomized protocols, in a recent work by Davies [12], a protocol that simulates a CONGEST round in a Beeping Network with O(∆2 log n) overhead is presented. The protocol works even in the presence of random noise in the communication channel. Still, it is correct only with high probability (whp),4 and requires a polynomial number of CONGEST rounds in the simulated algorithm. More relevant for comparison with our work, in the same paper, a lower bound of Ω(∆2 log n) on the overhead to simulate a CONGEST round is shown. The lower bound applies even in a noiseless environment and regardless of randomization. Thus, our simulation is optimal modulo some poly-logarithmic factor. [12] also proposed using superimposed codes instead of randomness, resulting in Θ(∆) multiplicative overhead, i.e., O(∆3 log n) rounds in total. Another randomized simulation was previously presented in [3] with overhead of O(∆ min(n, ∆2) log n) whp. Regarding the MIS problem, the closest work is the deterministic protocol in [5], which runs in O(∆2 log n + ∆3). Thus, our results improve by a factor of ∆/ polylog n for any ∆ ∈ ω(log n), and match the running time (modulo poly-logarithmic factor) for ∆ ∈ O(log n). On the side of randomized MIS protocols, an upper bound of O(log3 n) has been shown in [1] for the same beeping model, and faster with some additional assumptions. Our network decomposition algorithm for BNs is based on the protocol for the CONGEST model [20] that shows a (log n, log2 n) network decomposition in O(log5 n) CONGEST rounds. We get the same network decomposition in O(∆2 log7 n) beeping rounds. Other graph problems and (restricted) local broadcast of same message are discussed in the full version of this work [19]. Non-synchronized BNs pose even more challenges to graph algorithms, cf. [1, 18, 14, 24]. 2 Model, Notation, and Problems We specify first the communication network notation used throughout, and we define the communication models studied in the section that follows. We consider a communication network formed by n devices with communication and computation capabilities, called nodes. Each node has a unique ID from the range [1, nc] for some constant c ≥ 1.5 Nodes communicate by sending messages among them. A message is composed of a binary sequence containing the source node ID, the destination node ID (if applicable), and the specific information to be sent. If the destination node receives the message from the source node, 4 An event E occurs with high probability if P rob(E) ≥ 1 − 1/nc for some c > 0. 5 The availability of identifiers is essential in order to break symmetry in deterministic protocols, as pointed out in previous works on deterministic protocols in the Beeping model [5, 14]. P. Garncarek, D. R. Kowalski, S. Kutten, and M. A. Mosteiro 20:5 we say that the message was delivered. Each pair of nodes that are able to communicate directly (i.e., without relaying communication through other nodes) are said to be connected by a communication link and are called neighbors. We assume that links are symmetric, i.e., messages can be sent in both directions (delivery is restricted to the communication models specified below). The network topology defined by the communication links is modeled with an undirected graph G = (V, E) where V is the set of nodes and E is the set of links. If E is such that for every pair of nodes u, v ∈ V there is a path of links connecting u and v we say that the network is connected. For each node v ∈ V , the set of neighbors of v is called its neighborhood, denoted as N(v). We assume that time is slotted in rounds of communication. All nodes start running protocols simultaneously, i.e., the network is synchronous. We assume that computations take negligible time with respect to communication. Thus, we measure algorithm performance in rounds. 2.1 Communication Models Beeping Networks (BNs) [10]. In this model, in each round each node can either beep (send a signal) or listen (do not send any signal). By doing so, nodes obtain the following communication channel feedback. In any given round, a listening node hears either silence (no neighbor beeps) or noise (one or more neighbors beep). A listening node that hears noise cannot distinguish between a single beep and multiple beeps. Network protocols may use the channel feedback (i.e. the temporal sequence of strings from {“silence”,“noise”}) to make decisions adaptively. However, delivering messages is not straightforward because it requires sending (and receiving) the whole binary sequence of the message (according to some beeping schedule, possibly changing adaptively during the communication), somehow encoded with beeps. In that sense, protocols for Beeping Networks can be seen as radio network coding to cope with the communication restrictions (as in [16] to cope with noise). (a) All nodes listen. All nodes hear silence. (b) Node a beeps, {b, c, d, e, f} listen. {b, c, d} hear noise. {e, f} hear silence. (c) Nodes {a, b} beep, {c, d, e, f} listen. {c, d} hear noise. {e, f} hear silence. Figure 1 Beeping Network communication model example. CONGEST Networks [31]. In this model, in each round each node can send a (possibly different) message of O(log n) bits to each neighbor independently. All nodes receive the messages sent by their neighbors. That is, there are no collisions. In the CONGEST Broadcast version of this model, each node can only broadcast the same message to all its neighbors in each round. ESA 2025 20:6 Beeping Deterministic CONGEST Algorithms in Graphs 2.2 Problems Studied Our main research question in this work is how to efficiently simulate a round of communica-tion of a CONGEST Network protocol in a Beeping Network, and how to improve MIS in BNs. CONGEST Simulation. Given a Beeping Network with topology graph G = (V, E), where each node u ∈ V may hold a message mu,v of O(log n) bits that must be delivered to node v ∈ N(u), the CONGEST simulation problem is solved once for every u ∈ V and v ∈ N(u), mu,v and the ID of u has been delivered to v. Maximal Independent Set (MIS). Given a Beeping Network with topology graph G = (V, E), The Maximal Independent Set problem is solved when, for some such set, S ⊆ V , every node v ∈ S, v knows that it is in S. Our second main problem – generalized h-hop simulations – are defined and studied in Section 4 and in the full version of this work [19]. We also study several distributed computing problems of independent interest (see their definition in the full version of this work [19]) in the context of Beeping Networks and applications of our simulator that improve efficiency with respect to known solutions for BNs. 3 Simulation of a CONGEST Round in Beeping Networks We present a deterministic distributed algorithm that efficiently simulates a round of any algorithm designed for the CONGEST model in the Beeping Networks model, even if the algorithm sends different messages to neighbors. It is only somewhat (polylogarithmically) slower than the existing more restricted local broadcast algorithms, which require a node to send the same message to all its neighbors), e.g., the local broadcast solution mentioned in [12] (see also more details in the full version of this work [19]) and faster nearly by factor ∆ than the full deterministic simulation also mentioned in [12]. Unlike the superimposed-codes-based deterministic algorithms in [12], our simulator is adaptive and uses heavier machinery. It is built hierarchically using the known family of codes called “avoiding selectors”. The codes, intuitively, say “when to beep”. However, it is still possible that when two neighbors of some node v send their messages (by beeping in subsequent rounds), node v will receive a “message” that is a logical OR of the two. Our simulator resolves such collision problems and assures fast progress by: coding messages (cf. extended-IDs) such that OR of more than one message is identified; beeping according to specific avoiding selectors, assuring that a fraction of links that have not yet “delivered” the beeped messages will now succeed in one direction (i.e., one end node of such link “announces” itself to the other one through successful beeping of its ID); allowing those who received such announcements, the responders, to compete in successful delivery of their IDs to the corresponding announcers; this competition sets up bi-directional matching between the announcers and the corresponding responders, which allows bi-directional message exchange via beeping (3-stage parallel handshaking); this competition also uses avoiding selectors, but differently parameterized than the ones used for announcing (as announcing and responding are different processes). The announcing, followed by responders’ competition, continues until all links end up in some of the successfully realized matchings (as described in the above bullet points). Avoiding selectors for n nodes use two parameters, k, ℓ, corresponding to the number of competing neighbors/responders versus the other (potentially interrupting) neighbors: P. Garncarek, D. R. Kowalski, S. Kutten, and M. A. Mosteiro 20:7 Algorithm 1 c2b algorithm for each node u. E(u) is a global variable. E(u)← {{u, v}|{u, v} ∈ E for some v ∈ V } Algorithm C2B() 1 for each epoch i = 1, 2, . . . , log ∆ do // iterating epochs 2 ki ← ∆/2i 3 for each phase j = 1, 2, . . . , |F∆,ki | do 4 if u ∈ F∆,ki (j) then 5 announcer(u, ki) 6 else 7 responder(u, ki) ▶ Definition 1 (Avoiding selectors). A family F of subsets of [n] is called an (n, k, ℓ)-avoiding selector, where 1 ≤ ℓ < k ≤ n, if for every non-empty subset S ⊆ [n] such that |S| ≤ k and for any subset R ⊆ S of size at most ℓ, there is an element a ∈ S \R for which there exists a set F ∈ F such that |F ∩ S| = {a}. The following fact follows directly from Definition 1, see also [6, 9]. ▶ Fact 1. Suppose we are given an (n, k, ℓ)-avoiding selector F and a set S of size at most k. Then, the number of elements in S not “selected” by selector F (i.e., for which there is no set in the selector that intersects S on such singleton element) is smaller than k − ℓ. ▶ Theorem 2 ([6, 9]). There exists an (n, k, ℓ)-avoiding selector of size O ( k2 k−ℓ log n ) , and moreover, an (n, k, ℓ)-avoiding selector of size O ( k2 k−ℓ polylog n ) can be efficiently deterministically constructed (in polynomial time of n) for some polylogarithmic function polylog n, locally by each node. 3.1 The c2b Algorithm The pseudocode of our main c2b algorithm is in Algorithm 1, and its subroutines in Algorithms 2 and 3. The algorithm proceeds in epochs i = 1, . . . log ∆. In the beginning, each node has all its links not successfully realized – here by a link {v, w} being realized we understand that up to the current round, an input message/ID sent by v has been successfully encoded by w (using a sequence of beeps) and vice versa (note that these are two different messages and were sent/encoded each in different rounds); the formal definition of link realization will be given later. The goal of the algorithm is to preserve the following invariant for epoch i ≥ 1: At the end of epoch i = 1, . . . , log ∆, each vertex has less than κi = ∆/2i incident links not realized. We set an auxiliary value κ0 = ∆, which corresponds to the maximum number of adjacent links per node at the beginning of the computation. For ease of presentation, we assume that node IDs come from the range [1, n]. Note that in all the formulas, the number of possible IDs appears only under logarithms, so the algorithm and analysis for range [1, nc] are the same. ESA 2025 20:8 Beeping Deterministic CONGEST Algorithms in Graphs Algorithm for epoch i: Preliminaries and main concepts Epoch i proceeds in subsequent batches of 2 log n rounds, each batch is called a super-round. In a single super-round, a node can constantly listen or keep beeping according to some 0-1 sequence of length 2 log n, where 1 corresponds to beeping in the related round and 0 means staying silent. The sequences that the nodes use during the algorithm are extended-IDs, defined as follows: the first log n positions contain an ID of some node in {1, . . . , n}, while the next log n positions contain the same ID with the bits flipped, that is, with ones swapped to zeros and vice versa. Note that extended-IDs are pairwise different, and each of them contains exactly log n ones and log n zeros. We say that a node v beeps an extended-ID of node w in a super-round s if, within super-round s, node v beeps only in rounds corresponding to positions with 1’s in the extended ID of w (w could be a different node id than v). We say that a node w receives an extended-ID of a node v in a super-round s if: w does not beep in super-round s, the sequence of noise/silence heard by w in super-round s form an extended-ID of v. From the perspective of receiving information in a super-round, all other cases not falling under the above definition of receiving an extended-ID, i.e., when a node is not silent in the super-round or receives a sequence of beeps that does not form any extended-ID, are ignored by the algorithm, in the sense that it could be treated as meaningless information noise. Analogously to extended-ID’s, nodes create an extended-message by taking the binary representation of the message of logarithmic length and transforming it to a 2 log n binary sequence in the same way as an extended-ID is created from the binary ID of a node. An extended-message, as well as an extended-ID, is easily decodable after being received without interruptions from other neighbors. A specification of the conditions to achieve one-to-one communication, which is given “for free” in the CONGEST model, is crucial. An illustration of the following handshake communication procedure is shown in the full version of this work [19]. We say that our algorithm realizes link {v, w} if the following are satisfied: (a) there are three consecutive super-rounds (called “responding”) in which v beeps an extended-ID of itself followed by an extended-ID of w and then by extended-message of v addressed to w, and w receives them in these super-rounds; intuitively, it corres-ponds to the situation when v “tells” w that it dedicates these three super-rounds for communication from itself to w, and w receives this information; (b) there are three consecutive super-rounds (called “confirming”) in which w beeps an extended-ID of itself followed by an extended-ID of v and by its extended-message addressed to v, and v receives them in these super-rounds; intuitively, it corresponds to the situation when w “tells” v that it dedicates these three super-rounds for communication from itself to v, and v receives this information; (c) there is a super-round, not earlier than the one specified in point (a), at the end of which node w locally marks link {v, w} as realized, and analogously, there is a super-round, not earlier than the one specified in point (b), at the end of which node v locally marks link {v, w} as realized. It is straightforward to see that in super-rounds specified in points (a) and (b), a multi-directional communication between v and w takes place – by sending and receiving both “directed pairs” of extended-IDs of these two nodes, each of them commits that the super-rounds specified in points (a) and (b) are dedicated for sending a message dedicated to the other node, and vice versa. Additionally, in some super-round(s) both nodes commit that it has happened (c.f., point (c) above). P. Garncarek, D. R. Kowalski, S. Kutten, and M. A. Mosteiro 20:9 Algorithm 2 c2b algorithm for announcer node v. Procedure announcer(v, ki) // announcing super-round 1 for each round r = 1, 2, . . . , 2 log n do 2 if ⟨v⟩(r) = 1 then beep else listen 3 for each sub-phase a = 1, 2, . . . , log ki do 4 for b = 1, 2, . . . , |Fki/2a−2,ki/2a−1 | do // responding 3 super-rounds 5 for 6 log n rounds do listen // announcer only listens 6 if some ⟨w⟩⟨v⟩⟨mw,v⟩ was heard and {w, v} ∈ E(v) then // confirming 3 super-rounds 7 for each round r = 1, 2, . . . , 2 log n do 8 if ⟨v⟩(r) = 1 then beep else listen 9 for each round r = 1, 2, . . . , 2 log n do 10 if ⟨w⟩(r) = 1 then beep else listen 11 for each round r = 1, 2, . . . , 2 log n do 12 if ⟨mv,w⟩(r) = 1 then beep else listen 13 E(v)← E(v) \ {w, v} // link realized 14 if E(v) = ∅ then v stops executing 15 else 16 for 6 log n rounds do listen // wait to synchronize Algorithm for epoch i: Structure An epoch i is split into |F∆,ki | phases, for a given (n, ∆, ∆ − ki)-avoiding selector F∆,ki and parameter ki = ∆/2i, parameterized by a variable j. Each phase starts with one announcing super-round, in which nodes in set F∆,ki (j) beep in pursuit to be received by some of their neighbors. This super-round is followed by log ki sub-phases, parameterized by a = 1, . . . , log ki. A sub-phase a uses sets from an (n, ki/2a−2, ki/2a−1)-avoiding selector Fki/2a−2,ki/2a−1 to determine who beeps in which super-round (together with additional rules to decide what extended-ID and extended-message to beep and how to confirm receiving them), and consists of |Fki/2a−2,ki/2a−1 | 6-tuples of super-rounds (3 responding super-rounds and 3 confirming super-rounds). The goal of a phase is to realize links that were successfully received (“announced”) in the first (announcing) super-round of this phase. This is particularly challenging in a distributed setting since many neighbors could receive such an announcement, but the links between them and the announcing node must be confirmed so that one-to-one communication between the announcer and responders could take place in different super-rounds (in one super-round, a node can receive only logarithmic-size information). Definitions and notation. F∆,ki is a locally computed (n, ∆, ∆ − ki)-avoiding selector, and for any a = 1, . . . , log ki, Fki/2a−2,ki/2a−1 is a (locally computed) (n, ki/2a−2, ki/2a−1)-avoiding selector, as in Theorem 2. We denote the extended-ID of node x as ⟨x⟩, and the extended-message of node x for node y as ⟨mx,y⟩, both given as a sequence of bits. For any sequence of bits s, s(i) is the ith bit of s. ESA 2025 20:10 Beeping Deterministic CONGEST Algorithms in Graphs Algorithm 3 c2b algorithm for responder node w. Procedure responder(w, ki) 1 status(u)← nil // announcing super-round 2 for 2 log n rounds do listen // responder only listens 3 if some ⟨v⟩ was heard and {v, w} ∈ E(w) then 4 status(w)← v-responsive 5 for each sub-phase a = 1, 2, . . . , log ki do 6 for b = 1, 2, . . . , |Fki/2a−2,ki/2a−1 | do 7 if status(w) = v-responsive and w ∈ Fki/2a−2,ki/2a−1(b) then // responding 3 super-rounds 8 for each round r = 1, 2, . . . , 2 log n do 9 if ⟨w⟩(r) = 1 then beep else listen 10 for each round r = 1, 2, . . . , 2 log n do 11 if ⟨v⟩(r) = 1 then beep else listen 12 for each round r = 1, 2, . . . , 2 log n do 13 if ⟨mw,v⟩(r) = 1 then beep else listen // confirming 3 super-rounds 14 for 6 log n rounds do listen // responder only listens 15 if some ⟨v⟩⟨w⟩⟨mv,w⟩ was heard then 16 status(w)← nil 17 E(w)← E(w) \ {v, w} // link realized 18 if E(w) = ∅ then w stops executing 19 else 20 for 12 log n rounds do listen // wait to synchronize 3.2 Analysis of the c2b Algorithm Recall that the algorithm proceeds in synchronized super-rounds, each containing a subsequent 2 log n rounds. Therefore, our analysis assumes that the computation is partitioned into consecutive super-rounds and, unless stated otherwise, it focuses on correctness and progress in super-rounds. Recall also that each node either stays silent (no beeping at all) or beeps an extended ID of some node or an extended message of one node addressed to one of its neighbors in a super-round. The missing proofs are deferred to the full version of this work [19]. In the next two technical results, we state and prove the facts that receiving an extended-ID by a node w in a super-round can happen if and only if there is exactly one neighbor of w has been beeping the same extended-ID during the considered super-round. ▶ Fact 2 (Single beeping). If during a super-round, exactly one neighbor of a node w beeps an extended-ID of some z, then w receives this extended-ID in this super-round. Proof. Directly from the definition of receiving an extended-ID. ◀ P. Garncarek, D. R. Kowalski, S. Kutten, and M. A. Mosteiro 20:11 ▶ Lemma 3 (Correct receiving). During the algorithm, if a node w receives some extended-ID of z in a super-round, then some unique neighbor v of w has been beeping an extended-ID of z in this super-round while all other neighbors of w have been silent. The above holds except, possibly, some second responding super-rounds, in which a node can receive an extended-ID of z that has been beeped by more than one neighbor. We now prove that link realization implemented by our algorithm is consistent with the definition – it allocates in a distributed way super-rounds for bi-directional communication of distinct messages. ▶ Lemma 4 (Correct realization). If a node v (locally) marks some link {v, w} as realized, which may happen only at the end of a second confirming super-round, the link has been realized by then. As mentioned earlier in the description of the phase, the goal of a phase j (of epoch i) is to assure that any node v that was received by some other nodes w in the announcing super-round, gets all such links {v, w} realized by the end of the phase (and vice versa, because the condition on the realization by this algorithm is symmetric). The next step is conditional progress in a sub-phase a of a phase j. ▶ Lemma 5 (Sub-phase progress). Consider any node v and suppose that in the beginning of sub-phase a of phase j, there are at most ∆/2i+a−2 nodes w such that w is (j, v)-responsive and it does not mark link {v, w} as realized. Then, by the end of the sub-phase, the number of such nodes is reduced to less than ∆/2i+a−1. ▶ Lemma 6 (Phase progress). Consider a phase j of epoch i and assume that in the beginning, there are at most 2ki non-realized incident links to any node. Every node w that becomes (j, v)-responsive in the first (announcing) super-round of the phase, for some v, mark locally the link {v, w} as realized during this phase. And vice versa, also node v marks locally that link as realized. The next lemma proves the invariant for epoch i, assuming that it holds in the previ-ous epochs. ▶ Lemma 7 (Epoch invariant). The invariant for epoch i ≥ 1 holds. ▶ Theorem 8. The c2b algorithm deterministically and distributedly simulates any round of any algorithm designed for the CONGEST networks in O(∆2 polylog n log ∆) beeping rounds, where the polylog n is the square of the (poly-)logarithm in the construction of avoiding-selectors in Theorem 2 multiplied by log n. Proof. By Lemma 7, each epoch i reduces by at least half the number of non-realized incident links. We next count the number of rounds in each epoch by counting the number of super-rounds and multiplying the result by the O(log n) length of each super-round. Recall that link realization means that some triples of responding and confirming super rounds were not interrupted by other neighbors of both end nodes of that link; therefore, the attached extended messages (in the third super-rounds in a row) were correctly received. Thus, the local exchange of messages addressed to specific neighbors took place successfully. Each sub-phase a has O(∆2 polylog n) super-rounds, because for each set in of the (n, ki/2a−1, ki/2a)-avoiding selector Fki/2a−1,ki/2a , there are four super-rounds and the se-lector itself has O((ki/2a) polylog n) set, by Theorem 2. ESA 2025 20:12 Beeping Deterministic CONGEST Algorithms in Graphs Therefore, the total number of super-rounds in all sub-phases executed within the loops in Line 3 of Algorithm 2 and Line 5 of Algorithm 3 is O( log ki∑ a=1 (ki/2a) polylog n) ≤ O(ki polylog n) . Within one phase, they are executed as many times as the number of announcing super-rounds. The number of such super-rounds in a phase is |F∆,ki |, which is O((∆2/ki) polylog n) by Thm. 2. Thus, the total number of super-rounds in a phase is O((∆2/ki) polylog n · ki polylog n) ≤ O(∆2 polylog n), where the final polylog n is a square of the (poly-)log in Thm. 2. Since there are log ∆ epochs, the total number of super-rounds is O(∆2 polylog n log ∆), which is additionally multiplied by O(log n) – the length of each super-round – if we want to refer the total number of beeping rounds. ◀ Maximal Independent Set (MIS). To demonstrate that the above efficient simulator can yield efficient results for many graph problems, we apply it to the O(log5 n) MIS algorithm in [20] to improve polynomially (with respect to ∆) the best-known solutions for MIS (c.f. [5]): ▶ Corollary 9. MIS can be solved deterministically on any network of maximum node-degree ∆ in O(∆2 polylog n) beeping rounds. 4 Multi-hop Simulation We generalize the simulation at distance 1 to the following B-bit h-hop simulation problem: each node has messages, potentially different, of size at most B bits addressed to any other node, and it needs to deliver them to all destination nodes within distance at most h hops. If each node has only a single message of size at most B bits to be delivered to all nodes within distance at most h hops, then we call this restricted version B-bit h-hop Local Broadcast. Note also that we do not require messages addressed to nodes of distance larger than h to be delivered. Below we generalize the lower bound for single-hop simulation in [12] to multi-hop simulation and multi-hop local broadcast. ▶ Theorem 10. There is an adversarial network of size Θ(∆h) such that any B-bit h-hop simulation algorithm requires Ω(B∆h+1) beeping rounds to succeed with probability more than 2− 1 2 ·B(∆−1)h−2(∆/2)3 = 2−Θ(B∆h+1). Proof. Problem instance. We describe the construction of the adversarial network and input set of messages used to prove our lower bound as follows (refer to Figures 2 and 3). Consider a full bipartite graph K∆/2,∆/2, with one part called T and the other R. We focus on transmissions going towards nodes in R, hence nodes in R will be called receivers, while nodes in T = T1 will be called the first of h layers of transmitters. Each node in T1 will be a root of an (h−1)-depth tree of transmitters. We create a second layer of transmitters T2 composed of (∆/2)2 nodes. Each node in T1 (already connected to each node in R) will also be connected to different ∆/2 nodes in T2. For subsequent layers of transmitters, that is each layer Ti, for 3 ≤ i ≤ h, will be composed of (∆/2)2(∆− 1)i−2 nodes. Each node in layer Ti−1, for 3 ≤ i ≤ h, will be connected to different ∆− 1 nodes in layer Ti. Note that each node in the defined network has at most ∆ neighbors. P. Garncarek, D. R. Kowalski, S. Kutten, and M. A. Mosteiro 20:13 ∆ 2 ∆ 2 ∆ − 1 ∆ − 1∆ − 1 RT1T2T3T4Th Th−1 Figure 2 An illustration of the structure of the graph. The graph is partitioned into vertical layers Th, . . . , T1, R. The graph is branching out heavily, so we show only a path from an arbitrary node in T1 layer to an arbitrary node in Th layer, with all the edges incident to the path. The numbers between the layers denote the number of edges between the layers that are incident to the path or on the path. Recall that layers T1 and R have ∆/2 nodes each, while the other layers have significantly more nodes, but we only show nodes that are adjacent to the considered path. Figure 3 Illustration for Theorem 10. Example of adversarial graph for ∆ = 4 and h = 5. We define now the input set of messages as follows. Let each node v ∈ Th have a B-bit message mv→u to each node u ∈ R. We choose those messages uniformly at random. We will show that just these messages cannot be relayed efficiently and we do not need any other messages in our problem instance.6 Multihop Simulation. Here we analyze the multihop simulation algorithms. There are (∆/2)2(∆− 1)h−2 nodes in Th, and each of them has ∆/2 (possibly different) messages, one for each node in R. Therefore, there are (∆/2)3(∆ − 1)h−2 = Θ(∆h+1) messages to nodes in R that are passing through nodes in T1. Let R be the concatenated string of local randomness in all the nodes in R. The output of any receiver u ∈ R must depend only on R, node IDs and the pattern of beeps and silences of nodes in T1. There are 2t possible patterns of beeps and silences in t rounds. Therefore, the output of nodes in R must be one of the 2t possible distributions, where a distribution is over the randomness of R. The correct output of nodes in R is a string {0, 1}B(∆−1)h−2(∆/2)3 = {0, 1}Θ(B∆h+1) chosen uniformly at random (since the input messages of nodes in Th were chosen uniformly at random). Therefore, the probability of picking the correct result is at most 2t−B(∆−1)h−2(∆/2)3 , and any algorithm that finishes within t ≤ 1 2 ·B(∆− 1)h−2(∆/2)3 rounds has at most 2− 1 2 ·B(∆−1)h−2(∆/2)3 probability of outputting the correct answer. ◀ 6 Alternatively we can make all other messages known to the optimal algorithm, e.g., by setting them to 0B . ESA 2025 20:14 Beeping Deterministic CONGEST Algorithms in Graphs Algorithm A simple algorithm would repeatedly use a 1-hop Local Broadcast routine to flood the network with the messages until nodes at a distance h received the messages. This, however, can take Ω(∆2h) rounds. Instead, we limit the flooding by only sending messages along the shortest paths to their destinations using a 1-hop simulation algorithm. The details of the algorithm as well as its analysis are presented next. In the beginning, nodes use a standard protocol to disseminate their IDs up to distance h. They do it in h subsequent epochs, each epoch i of ti rounds sufficient to run our Local Broadcast (see full version of this work [19]) for messages of size ∆i log n. These messages contain different IDs learned by the node at the beginning of the current epoch. A direct inductive argument, also using the property that there are at most ∆i nodes at a distance at most i, shows that at the end of epoch i, each node knows the IDs of all nodes at a distance at most i from it. Additionally, each node records in which epoch i it learned each known ID v for the first time and from which of its neighbors w – and stores this information as a triple (v, w, i). The invariant for i = h proves that at the end of epoch h, each node knows IDs of all nodes of distance at most h from it. The round complexity is∑h i=1 ∆i log n ·∆2 log n = O(∆h+2 log2 n), and as will be seen later, it is subsumed by the round complexity of the second part of our algorithm (as the polylog n function in Theorem 8 is asymptotically bigger than log2 n). Note that a sequence of triples (v, w1 = v, 1), . . . , (v, wℓ, ℓ), stored at nodes w2, w3, . . . , wℓ, wℓ+1 = u respectively, represents a shortest path to node v starting from the node u; the length of that path is ℓ. In the second part, nodes also proceed in epochs, but this time each epoch i takes t∗ i rounds sufficient to execute 1-hop simulation algorithm from Section 3 (see Theorem 8) for point-to-point messages of size (B + log n)∆h. Here B denotes the known upper bound on the size of any input message. In epoch i, every node u transmits a (possibly different) message of size (B + log n)∆h to each neighbor w. Such a message contains all the input messages of nodes within i − 1 distance and the recipients of these messages such that w is the next node on the saved shortest path to the recipient. The messages have already traveled i− 1 hops, so their destination is at most h− (i− 1) hops away. More specifically, the message from node u addressed to a neighbor w in epoch i contains pairs (v, mz→v), where v is such that (v, w, i′) is stored at the node for some i′ ≤ h− (i− 1) and mz→v is a message received by the node u in epoch i− 1 (in case of i− 1 = 0, it is the original message of the node addressed to v). A direct inductive argument shows that at the end of each epoch i a node knows at most ∆i messages addressed to any node v of distance ℓ ≤ h− i from the node. This invariant is based on the following arguments: Because there is a unique neighbor w of the node u such that a triple (v, w, ℓ) is stored at the node, the number of such nodes v of distance at most h− i + 1 from the node u is at most ∆h−i+1, by the end of epoch i− 1, node u could receive messages to be relayed to v from ∆i−1 different nodes at distance i− 1, each message contains up to B-bit long original message and an ID of length log n, hence, messages of size at most (B + log n)∆i−1 ·∆h−i+1 = (B + log n)∆h are being sent to each neighbor in epoch i, and by definition – epoch i has sufficient number of rounds to deliver them. P. Garncarek, D. R. Kowalski, S. Kutten, and M. A. Mosteiro 20:15 The invariant for i = h proves the desired property of B-bit h-hop simulation. The total number of rounds is O(h · (B + log n)∆h ·∆2 polylog n) ⊆ O(h · B∆h+2 polylog n), where factor h comes from the number of epochs, each sending at most ∆ point-to-point messages of size at most (B + log n)∆h to neighbors (by the invariant) using the 1-hop simulation protocol with overhead O(∆2 polylog n) (by Theorem 8). Hence we proved the following. ▶ Theorem 11. There is a distributed deterministic algorithm solving the B-bit h-hop simulation problem in a beeping network in O(h ·B∆h+2 polylog n) rounds. 5 Conclusions We provided deterministic distributed algorithms to efficiently simulate a round of algorithms designed for the CONGEST model on the Beeping Networks. This allowed us to improve polynomially the time complexity of several (also graph) problems on Beeping Networks. The first simulation by the Local Broadcast algorithm is shorter by a polylogarithmic factor than the other, more general one – yet still powerful enough to implement some algorithms, including the prominent solution to Network Decomposition [20]. The more general one could be used for solving problems such as MIS. We also considered efficient pipelining of messages via several layers of BN. Two important lines of research arise from our work. First, whether some (graph) problems do not need local broadcast to be solved deterministically, and whether their time complexity could be asymptotically below ∆2. Second, could a lower bound on any deterministic local broadcast algorithm, better than Ω(∆ log n), be proved? Building an eBPF/XDP NAT-Based Layer 4 Load Balancer from Scratch Building an eBPF/XDP NAT-Based Layer 4 Load Balancer from Scratch Tutorial by Teodor Janez Podobnik Sale ends in 8 days Sale — 50% OFF Sign in Sign up Table of contents NAT (Network Address Translation) and eBPF Load Balancing Rewriting IP and MAC Addresses Running the Load Balancer Start tutorial Reset Progress Are you sure you want to reset your progress for this tutorial?
